PREFACE 

Tue Quran 1s a book which emphasizes ‘deed’ rather 
than ‘idea’ There are, however, men to whom it 1s 
not possible organically to assimilate an alien uni- 
verse by re-living, as a vital process, that special 
type of inner experience on which religious faith 
ultimately rests Moreover, the modern man, by 
developing habits of concrete thought—habits 
which Islam itself fostered at least in the earlier 
stages of 1ts cultural career—has rendered himself 
less capable of that experience which he further 
suspects because of its lability to illusion The 
more genuine schools of Sufiism have, no doubt, 
done good work 1n shaping and directing the evolu- 
tion of religious experience in Islam, but their 
latter-day representatives, owing to their ignorance 
of the modern mind, have become absolutely 1n- 
capable of receiving any fresh inspiration from 
modern thought and experience They are per- 
petuating methods which were created for genera- 
tions possessing a cultural outlook differing, 1n 
important respects, from our own ‘Your creation 
and resurrection,’ says the Quran, ‘are like the 
creation and resurrection of a single soul’ A living 
experience of the kind of biological unity, embodied 
in this verse, requires to-day a method physio- 
logically less violent and psychologically more 
suitable to a concrete type of mind In the 
absence of such a method the demand for a 
scientific form of religious knowledge 1s only 
natural In these lectures, which were undertaken 
at the request of the Madras Muslim Association 
and delivered at Madras, Hyderabad, and Aligarh, 
TI have tried to meet, even though partially, this 
urgent demand by attempting to reconstruct Mus- 
lim religious philosophy with due regard to the 
philosophical traditions of Islam and the more 

Preface 

recent developments in the various domains of 
human knowledge And the present moment ts 
quite favourable for such an undertaking Classical 
Physics has learned to criticize 1ts own foundations 
As a result of this criticism the kind of materialism, 
which it originally necessitated, 1s rapidly dis- 
appearing, and the day 1s not far off when Religion 
and Science may discover hitherto unsuspected 
mutual harmonies It must, however, be remem- 
bered that there 1s no such thing as finality 1n 
philosophical thinking As knowledge advances 
and fresh avenues of thought are opened, other 
views, and probably sounder views than those set 
forth in these lectures, are possible Our duty 1s 
carefully to watch the progress of human thought, 
and to maintain an independent critical attitude 
towards it

KNOWLEDGE AND RELIGIOUS EXPERIENCE 

We is the character and general structure of 

the universe 1n which we live? Is there a 
permanent element in the constitution of this universe? 
How are we related to it® What place do we occupy 
in 1t, and what 1s the kind of conduct that befits the place 
we occupy® These questions are common to religion, 
philosophy, and higher poetry But the kind of 
knowledge that poetic inspiration brings 1s essentially 
individual in its character, it 1s figurative, vague, and 
indefinite Religion, in 1ts more advanced forms, rises 
higher than poetry It moves from individual to 
society In 1ts attitude towards the ultimate reality 1t 
18 opposed to the limitations of man, it enlarges his 
claims and holds out the prospect of nothing less than a 
direct vision of Reality Is1it then possible to apply the 
purely rational method of philosophy to religion? The 
spirit of philosophy 1s one of free inquiry It suspects 
all authority Its function 1s to trace the uncritical 
assumptions of human thought to their hiding places, 
and 1n this pursuit 1t may finally end in denial or a frank 
admission of the incapacity of pure reason to reach the 
ultimate reality The essence of religion, on the other 
hand, 1s faith, and faith, like the bird, sees 1ts ‘trackless 
way’ unattended by intellect which, in the words of the 
great mystic poet of Islam, ‘only waylays the living 
heart*of man and robs 1t of the invisible wealth of life 
that fies within’ Yet 1t cannot be demied that faith 1s 
more than mere feeling It has something hike a cogni- 
tive content, and the existence of rival parties—scholas- 
tics and mystics—in the history of religion shows that 
idea 1s a vital element 1n religion Apart from this, 
religion on 1ts doctrinal side, as defined by Professor 

2 Knowledge and Religious Experience 

Whitehead, 1s ‘a system of general truths which have 
the effect of transforming character when they are 
sincerely held and vividly apprehended’ Now, since 
the transformation and guidance of man’s inner and 
outer life is the essential aim of religion, it 1s Obvious 
that the general truths which it embodies must not 
remain unsettled No one would hazard action on the 
basis of a doubtful principle of conduct Indeed, in 
view of its function, religion stands in greater need of 
a rational foundation of its 1iltrmate principles than even 
the dogmas of science Science may ignore a rational 
metaphysics, indeed, it has ignored 1t so far Religion 
can hardly afford to ignore the search for a reconcilia- 
tion of the oppositions of experience and a justification 
of the environment in which humanity finds itself 
That 1s why Professor Whitehead has acutely remark- 
ed that ‘the ages of faith are the ages of rationalism ’ 
But to rationalize faith 1s not to admit the superiority 
of philosophy over religion Philosophy, no doubt, has 
jurisdiction to judge religion, but what 1s to be judged 
is of such a nature that it will not submit to the juris- 
diction of philosophy except on 1ts own terms While 
sitting in judgment on religion, philosophy cannot give 
religion an inferior place among its data Religion 1s 
not a departmental affair, it 1s neither mere thought, 
nor mere feeling, nor mere action, it 1s an expression of 
the whole man Thus, 1n the evaluation of religion, 
philosophy must recognize the central position of rel1- 
gion and has no other alternative but to admit it as 
something focal in the process of reflective synthesis 
Nor 1s there any reason to suppose that thought and 
intuition are essentially opposed to each other They 
spring up from the same root and complement each 
other The one grasps Reality piecemeal, the other 
grasps it in its wholeness The one fixes its gaze on the 
eternal, the other on the temporal aspect of Reality The 

Knowledge and Religious Experience 3 

one 1s present enjoyment of the whole of Reality, the 
other aims at traversing the whole by slowly specifying 
and closing up the various regions of the whole for ex- 
clusive observation Both are in need of each other for 
mutual rejuvenation Both seek visions of the same 
Reality which reveals itself to them 1n accordance with 
their function in life In fact, intuition, as Bergson 
rightly says, 1s only a higher kind of intellect 

The search for rational foundations in Islam may 
be regarded to have begun with the Prophet himself 
His constant prayer was ‘God! grant me knowledge of 
the ultimate nature of things’ The work of later 
mystics and non-mystic rationalists forms an exceed- 
ingly instructive chapter in the history of our culture, 
inasmuch as it reveals a longing for a coherent system 
of ideas, a spirit of whole-hearted devotion to truth, as 
well as the limitations of the age, which rendered the 
various theological movements in Islam less fruitful 
than they might have been 1n a different age As we 
all know, Greek philosophy has been a great cultural 
force 1n the history of Islam Yet a careful study of 
the Quran and the various schools of scholastic theo- 
logy that arose under the inspiration of Greek thought 
disclose the remarkable fact that while Greek phuilo- 
sophy very much broadened the outlook of Mushm 
thinkers, it, on the whole, obscured their vision of the 
Quran Socrates concentrated his attention on the 
human world alone To him the proper study of man 
was man and not the world of plants, insects, and stars 
How unlike the spirit of the Quran, which sees 1n the 
humble bee a recipient of Divine inspiration and con- 
stantly calls upon the reader to observe the perpetual 
change of the winds, the alternation of day and night, the 
clouds, the starry heavens, and the planets swimming 
through infinite space! As a true disciple of Socrates, 
Plato despised sense-perception which, in his view, 

4 Knowledge and Religious Experience 

yielded mere opinion and no real knowledge How un- 
like the Quran, which regards ‘hearing’ and ‘sight’ as 
the most valuable Divine gifts and declares them to be 
accountable to God for their activity in this world This 
1s what the earlier Muslim students of the Quran com- 
pletely missed under the spell of classical speculation 
They read the Quran in the light of Greek thought It 
took them over 200 years to perceive—though not quite 
clearly—that the spirit of the Quran was essentially 
anti-classical, and the result of this perception was a 
kind of intellectual revolt, the full significance of which 
has not been realized even up to the present day It was 
partly owing to this revolt and partly to his personal 
history that Ghazali based religion on philosophical 
scepticism—a rather unsafe basis for religion and not 
wholly justified by the spirit of the Quran Ghazalt’s 
chief opponent, Ibn-1-Rushd, who defended Greek phi- 
losophy against the rebels, was led, through Aristotle 
to what 1s known as the doctrine of Immortality of 
Active Intellect, a doctrine which once wielded enor- 
meus influence on the intellectual life of France and 
Italy, but which, to my mind, 1s entirely opposed to the 
view that the Quran takes of the value and destiny of 
the humanego Thus Ibn-1-Rushd lost sight of a great 
and fruitful 1dea in Islam and unwittingly helped the 
growth of that enervating philosophy of life which 
obscures man’s vision of himself, his God, and his 
world The more constructive among the Ash‘arite 
thinkers were no doubt on the night path and anticipat- 
ed some of the more modern forms of Idealism, yet, on 
the whole, the object of the Ash‘arite movement was 
simply to defend orthodox opinion with the weapons of 
Greek Dnalectic The Mutazila, conceiving religion 
merely as a body of doctrine and ignoring 1t as a vital 
fact, took no notice of non-conceptual modes of 
approaching Reality and reduced religion to a mere 

Knowledge and Religious Experience 5 

system of logical concepts ending in a purely negative 
attitude They failed to see that in the domain of 
knowledge—scientific or religious—complete indepen- 
dence of thought from concrete experience 1s not 
possible 

It cannot, however, be denied that Ghazali’s 
mission was almost apostolic like that of Kant in 
Germany of the eighteenth century In Germany 
rationalism appeared as an ally of religion, but she soon 
realized that the dogmatic side of religion was incapable 
of demonstration The only course open to her was to 
eliminate dogma from the sacred record With the 
elimination of dogma came the utilitarian view of 
morality, and thus rationalism completed the reign of 
unbelief Such was the state of theological thought in 
Germany when Kant appeared Huis Critique of Pure 
Reason revealed the limitations of human reason and 
reduced the whole work of the rationalists to a heap oi 
ruins And justly has he been described as God’s great- 
est gift to his country Ghazali’s philosophical scepti- 
cism which, however, went a little too far, virtually did 
the same kind of work in the world of Islam 1n break- 
ing the back of that proud but shallow rationalism 
which moved 1n the same direction as pre-Kantian 
rationalism in Germany There 1s, however, one 1m- 
portant difference between Ghazali1 and Kant Kant, 
consistently with his principles, could not affirm the 
possibility of a knowledge of God Ghazal1, finding no 
hope in analytic thought, moved to mystic experience, 
and there found an independent content for religion In 
this way he succeeded 1n securing for region the night 
to exist independently of science and metaphysics But 
the revelation of the total Infinite in mystic experience 
convinced him of the finitude and inconclusiveness of 
thought and drove him to draw a line of cleavage 
between thought and intuition He failed to see that 
thought and intu:tion are organically related and that 

6 Knowledge and Religious Experience 

thought must necessarily simulate finitude and incon- 
clusiveness because of 1ts alliance with serial tame The 
idea that thought 1s essentially finite, and for this reason 
unable to capture the Infinite, 1s based on a mistaken 
notion of the movement of thought in knowledge It 
is the inadequacy of the logical understanding which 
finds a multiplicity of mutually repellent individualities 
with no prospect of their ultimate reduction to a unity 
that makes us sceptical about the conclusiveness ot 
thought In fact, the logical understanding 1s incap- 
able of seeing this multiplicity as a coherent universe 
Its only method 1s generalization based on resembl- 
ances, but 1ts generalizations are only fictitious unities 
which do not affect the reality of concrete things In 
its deeper movement, however, thought 1s capable of 
reaching an immanent Infinite in whose self-unfolding 
movement the various finite concepts are merely 
moments In its essential nature, then, thought 1s not 
static, 1t 1s dynamic and unfolds its internal infinitude 
in time like the seed which, from the very beginning, 
carries within itself the organic unity of the tree as a 
present fact Thought 1s, therefore, the whole 1n 1ts 
dynamic self-expression, appearing to the temporal 
vision as a series of definite specifications which cannot 
be understood except by a reciprocal reference Their 
meaning lies not 1n their self-identity, but 1n the larger 
whole of which they are the specific aspects This larger 
whole 1s, to use a Quranic metaphor, a kind of ‘Preserv- 
ed Tablet’, which holds up the entire undetermined pos- 
sibilities of knowledge as a present reality, revealing 
itself in serial time as a succession of finite concepts 
appearing to reach a unity which 1s already present in 
them It 1s 1n fact the presence of the total Infinite 1n 
the movement of knowledge that makes finite thinking 
possible Both Kant and Ghazal: failed to see that 
thought, in the very act of knowledge, passes beyond 
its own finitude The finitudes of Nature are recipro- 

Knowledge and Religious Experience 2 

cally exclusive Not so the finitudes of thought which 
1S, n 1ts essential nature, incapable of limitation and 
cannot remain imprisoned 1n the narrow circuit of its 
own individuality In the wide world beyond itself 
nothing 1s alien to it It 1s 1n 1ts progressive participa- 
tion 1n the life of the apparently alien that thought de- 
molishes the walls of 1ts finitude and enjoys 1ts potential 
infinitude Its movement becomes possible onlv 
because of the implicit presence 1n 1ts finite individuality 
of the infinite, which keeps alive within 1t the flame of 
aspiration and sustains 1t 1n 1ts endless pursuit It1s a 
mistake to regard thought as inconclusive, for 1t too, mn 
its own way, 18 a greeting of the finite with the infinite 

During the last five hundred years religious thought 
in Islam has been practically stationary There was a 
time when European thought received inspiration from 
the world of Islam The most remarkable phenomenon 
of modern history, however, 1s the enormous rapidity 
with which the world of Islam 1s spiritually moving 
towards the West There 1s nothing wrong in this 
movement, for European culture, on its intellectual 
side, 1s only a further development of some of the most 
important phases of the culture of Islam Our only 
fear 1s that the dazzling exterior of European culture 
may arrest our movement and we may fail to reach the 
true inwardness of that culture During all the cen- 
turies of our intellectual stupor Europe has been 
seriously thinking on the great problems in which the 
philosophers and scientists of Islam were so keenly 
interested Since the Middle Ages when the schools 
of Muslim theology were completed, infinite advance 
has taken place in the domain of human thought and 
experience The extension of man’s power over nature 
has given him a new faith and a fresh sense of 
superiority over the forces that constitute his environ- 
ment New points of view have been suggested, old 

8 Knowledge and Religious Experience 

problems have been re-stated in the light of tresa 
experience, and new problems have arisen It seemis 
as 1f the intellect of man 1s outgrowing its own most 
fundamental categories—time, space, and causality. 
With the advance of scientific thought even our concept 
of intelligibility 1s undergoing a change The theory 
of Einstein has brought a new vision of the universe 
and suggests new ways of looking at the problems 
common to both religion and philosophy No wonder 
then that the younger generation of Islam in Asia and 
Africa demand a fresh orrentation of their faith With 
the reawakening of Islam, therefore, it 1s necessary to 
examine, in an independent spirit, what Europe nas 
thought and how far the conclusions reached by her can 
help us in the revision and, 1f necessary, reconstruction, 
of theological thought in Islam __ Besides this it is not 
possible to ignore the generally anti-religious and 
especially anti-Islamic propaganda in Central Asia 
which has already crossed the Indian frontier Some 
of the apostles of this movement are born Muslims, and 
one of them, Tawfik Fitrat, the Turkish poet, who died 
only a short time ago, has gone to the extent of using 
our great poet-thinker, Mirza Abdul Qadir Bedil of 
Akbarabad, for the purposes of this movement Surely, 
it 1s high time to look to the essentials of Islam In 
these lectures I propose to undertake a philosophical 
discussion of some of the basic ideas of Islam, in the 
hope that this may, at least, be helpful towards a proper 
understanding of the meaning of Islam as a message to 
humanity Also with a view to give a kind of ground- 
outline for further discussion, I propose, in this pre- 
liminary lecture, to consider the character of knowledge 
and religious experience 

The main purpose of the Quran 1s to awaken :n 
man the higher consciousness of his manifold relations 
with God and the universe. It 1s in view of this essen- 

Knowledge and Religious Experience 9 

tial aspect of the Quranic teaching that Goethe, while 
making a general review of Islam as an educational 
force, said to Eckermann ‘You see this teaching never 
fails with all our systems, we cannot go, and generally 
speaking no man can go, farther than that’ The 
problem of Islam was really suggested by the mutual 
conflict, and at the same time mutual attraction, 
presented by the two forces of religion and civilization 
The same problem confronted early Christianity The 
great point in Christianity 1s the search for an inde- 
pendent content for spiritual life which, according to 
the insight of 1ts founder, could be elevated, not by the 
forces of a world external to the soul of man, but bv the 
revelation of a new world within his soul Islam fully 
agrees with this insight and supplements it by the fur- 
ther insight that the illumination of the new world thus 
revealed 1s not something foreign to the world of 
matter but permeates it through and through 

Thus the affirmation of spirit sought by Christianity 
would come not by the renunciation of external forces 
which are already permeated by the illumination of 
spirit, but by a proper adjustment of man’s relation to 
these forces 1n view of the light received from the 
world within It 1s the mysterious touch of the 1deal 
that animates and sustains the real, and through it alone 
we can discover and afhrm the ideal With Islam the 
ideal and the real are not two opposing forces which 
cannot be reconciled The life of the 1deal consists, not 
in a total breach with the real which would tend to 
shatter the organic wholeness of life imto painful 
oppositions, but 1n the perpetual endeavour of the 1deal 
to appropriate the real with a view eventually to absorh 
it, to convert 1t into itself and to illuminate its whole 
being It 1s the sharp opposition between the subject 
and the obrect the mathematical without and the 
biological within that impressed Christiamity Islam, 

10 Knowledge and Religious Experience 

however, faces the opposition with a view to overcome 
it This essential difference 1n looking at a fundamental 
relation determines the respective attitudes of these 
great religions towards the problem of human life in 
its present surroundings Both demand the affirmation 
of the spiritual self in man, with this difference only 
that Islam, recognizing the contact of the ideal with 
the real, says ‘yes’ to the world of matter and points the 
way to master 1t with a view to discover a basis for a 
realistic regulation of life 

What, then, according to the Quran, 1s the charac- 
ter of the universe which we inhabit? In the first place, 
it 1s not the result of a mere creative sport 

‘We have not created the Heavens and the earth and what- 
ever 1s between them in sport We have not created them put 
for a serious end but the greater part of them understand it 

not’ (44 38) 

It 1s a reality to be reckoned with 

‘Verily in the creation of the Heavens and of the eartn, 
and in the succession of the night and of the day, are signs for 
men of understanding, who, standing and sitting and _ reclin- 
ing, bear God in mind and reflect on the creation of the 
Heavens and of the earth, and say “Oh our Lord! Thou 
hast not created this in vain” ’ (3 188) 

Again the universe 1s so constituted that 1t 1s capable of 
extension 
‘He (God) adds to His creation what He wills’ (35 1) 

It 1s not a block universe, a finished product immobile 
and incapable of change Deep 1n its inner being hes, 
perhaps the dream of a new birth 

‘Say—go through the earth and see how God hath brought

In fact, this mysterious swing and impulse of the unt- 
verse, this noiseless swim of time which appears to us, 
human beings, as the movement of day and night, 1s 
regarded by the Quran as one of the greatest signs of 
God 

‘God causeth the day and the night to take their turn 
Verily in this 1s teaching for men of insight’ (24 44) 

This 1s why the Prophet said ‘Do not vilify time, for 
time 1s God’ And this immensity of time and space 
carries 1n 1t the promise of a complete subjugation by 
man whose duty 1s to reflect on the signs of God, and

‘See ye not how God hath put under you all that 1s in the

‘And He hath subjected to you the night and the day, the

Such being the nature and promise of the universe, 
what 1s the nature of man whom it confronts on all 
sides?’ Endowed with a most suitable mutual adyjust- 
ment of faculties he discovers himself down below in 
the scale of life, surrounded on all sides by the forces 
of obstruction 

‘That of goodliest fabric We created man, then brought 
him down to the lowest of the low’ (95 4) 

And how do we find him in this environment? A ‘rest- 
less’ being engrossed 1n his ideals to the point of for- 
getting everything else, capable of inflicting pain on 
himself 1n his ceaseless quest after fresh scopes for selt- 
expression With all his failings he 1s superior to

His career, no doubt, has a beginning, but he 1s destined,

When attracted by the forces around him, man has the

It 1s the lot of man to share 1n the deeper aspirations of

energy to mould its forces to his own ends and pur-

No doubt, the immediate purpose of the Quran in 
this reflective observation of nature 1s to awaken in 
man the consciousness of that of which nature 1s 
regarded a symbol But the point to note 1s the general 
empirical attitude of the Quran which engendered 1n its 
followers a feeling of reverence for the actual and ulti- 
mately made them the founders of modern science It 
was a great point to awaken the empurical spirit in an 
age which renounced the visible as of no value in men’s 
search after God According to the Quran, as we have 
seen before, the universe has a serious end Its shift- 
ing actualities force our being into fresh formations 
The intellectual effort to overcome the obstruction

THE PHILOSOPHICAL TEST OF THE 
REVELATIONS OF RELIGIOUS

S CHOLASTIC Philosophy has put forward three 

arguments for the existence of God These 
arguments, known as the Cosmological, the Teleologi- 
cal, and the Ontological, embody a real movement of 
thought 1n its quest after the Absolute But regarded as 
logical proofs, I am afraid, they are open to serious 
criticism and further betray a rather superficial inter- 
pretation of experience 

The Cosmological argument views the world 
as a finite effect, and passing through a series of 
dependent sequences, related as causes and effects, 
stops at an uncaused first cause, because of the 
unthinkability of an infinite regress It 1s, however, 
obvious that a finite effect can only give a finite 
cause, or at most an infinite series of such causes 
To finish the series at a certain point, and to ele- 
vate one member of the series to the dignity of an 
uncaused first cause, 1s to set at naught the very law of 
causation on which the whole argument proceeds 
Further, the first cause reached by the argument neces- 
sarily excludes its effect And this means that the 
effect, constituting a limit to 1ts own cause, reduces tt 
to something finite Again the cause reached by the 
argument cannot be regarded as a necessary being, for 
the obvious reason that in the ‘relation of cause and 
effect the two terms of the relation are equally neces- 
sary to each other Nor 1s the necessity of existence 
identical with the conceptual necessity of causation 

30 The Revelations of Religious Experience 

which is the utmost that this argument can prove The 
argument really tries to reach the finite by merely 
negating the finite But the infinite reached by con- 
tradicting the finite 1s a false infinite, which neither 
explains itself nor the finite which 1s thus made to stand 
in Opposition to the infinite The true infinite does not 
exclude the finite, it embraces the finite without effac- 
ing its finitude, and explains and justifies its being 
Logically speaking, then, the movement from the finite 
to the infinite as embodied in the Cosmological argu- 
ment 1s quite illegitimate, and the argument fails im 
toto The Teleological argument is no better It scru- 
tinizes the effect with a view to discover the character 
of its cause From the traces of foresight, purpose, 
and adaptation in nature, it infers the existence of a selt- 
conscious being of infinite intelligence and power At 
best, it gives us a skilful external contriver working on 
a pre-existing dead and intractable material the ele- 
ments of which are, by their own nature, incapable of 
orderly structures and combinations The argument 
gives us a contriver only and not a creator, and even 1f 
we suppose him to be also the creator of his material, 1t 
does no credit to his wisdom to create his own difficul- 
ties by first creating intractable material, and then over- 
coming its resistance by the application of methods 
alien to its original nature The designer regarded as 
external to his material must always remain limited by 
his material, and hence a finite designer whose limited 
resources compel him to overcome his difficulties after 
the fashion of a human mechanician The truth 1s that 
the analogy on which the argument proceeds 1s of no 
value at all There is really no analogy between the 
work of the human artificer and the phenomena of 
nature The human artificer cannot work out his plan 
except by selecting and isolating his materials from 
their natural relations and situations Nature how- 

The Revelations of Religious Experience 31 

ever, constitutes a system of wholly interdependent 
members, her processes present no analogy to the 
architect’s work which, depending on a progressive 
isolation and integration of its material, can offer no 
resemblance to the evolution of organic wholes m 
nature The Ontological argument which has been 
presented in various forms by various thinkers has 
always appealed most to the speculative mind The 
Cartesian form of the argument runs thus 

‘To say that an attribute 1s contained in the nature or in 
the eoncept of a thing 1s the same as to say that the attribute 
is true of this thing and that it may be affirmed to be in it 
But necessary existence 1s contained in the nature or the 
concept of God Hence it may be with truth afhrmed that 
necessary existence 1s in God, or that God exists’ 

Descartes supplements this argument by another 
We have the 1dea of a perfect being 1n our mind What 
is the source of the idea? It cannot come from nature, 
for nature exhibits nothing but change It cannot 
create the idea of a perfect being Therefore corres- 
ponding to the 1dea 1n our mind there must be an obiec- 
tive counterpart which 1s the cause of the 1dea of a per- 
fect being in our mind This argument 1s somewhat of 
the nature of the Cosmological argument which I have 
already criticized But whatever may be the form of 
the argument, it 1s clear that the conception of exist- 
ence 1s no proof of objective existence Asin Kant’s 
criticism of this argument the notion of 300 dollars in 
my mind cannot prove that I have them in my pocket 
All that the argument proves 1s that the 1dea of a perfect 
being includes the zdea of his existence Between the 
idea of a perfect being in my mind and the objective 
reality of that being there 1s a gulf which cannot be 
bridged over by a transcendental act of thought The 
argument, as stated, 1s 1n fact a petitio principu, for 1t 

32 The Revelations of Religious Experience 

takes for granted the very point in question, 1¢, the 
transition from the logical to the real I hope I have 
made it clear to you that the Ontological and the Teleo- 
logical arguments, as ordinarily stated, carry us no- 
where And the reason of their failure is that they 
look upon ‘thought’ as an agency working on things 
from without This view of thought gives us a mere 
mechanician in the one case, and creates an unbridge- 
able gulf between the ideal and the real in the other It 
is, however, possible to take thought not as a principle 
which organizes and integrates 1ts material from the 
outside, but as a potency which 1s formative of the very 
being of 1ts material Thus regarded thought or 1dea 
is not alien to the original nature of things, 1t 1s their 
ultimate ground and constitutes the very essence of 
their being, infusing itself 1n them from the very begin- 
ning of their career and inspiring their onward march 
to a self-determined end But our present situation 
necessitates the dualism of thought and being Every act 
of human knowledge bifurcates what might on proper 
inquiry turn out to be a unity into a self that knows and 
a confronting ‘other’ that 1s known That 1s why we 
are forced to regard the object that confronts the self 
as something existing 1n its own right, external to and 
independent of the self whose act of knowledge makes 
no difference to the object known The true signifi- 
cance of the Ontological and the Teleological argu- 
ments will appear only if we are able to show that the 
human situation 1s not final and that thought and being 
are ultimately one This 1s possible only if we care- 
fully examine and interpret experience, following the

persisted in the history of thought and received the 
keenest attention from successive generations of 
thinkers Two of these arguments may be noted here 
Zeno, who took space to be infinitely divisible argued 
that movement in space 1s impossible Before the 
moving body can reach the point of its destination 1t 
must pass through half the space intervening between 
the point of start and the point of destination, and 
before 1t can pass through that half 1t must travel 
through the half of the half, and so on to infinity We 
cannot move from one point of space to another with-

intervening space But it 1s impossible to pass through 
an infinity of points ina finite time He further argued 
that the flying arrow does not move, because at any

point of space Thus Zeno held that movement 1s only

points in the series, 1t does mean that poimts are

‘Zeno asks—how can you go from one position at one

There is, however, one question which we must

‘And no one thing 1s here, but with Us are its store-houses,

The rise and growth of Atomism in Islam—the first 
important indication of an intellectual revolt against 
the Aristotelian idea of a fixed universe—forms one of 
the most interesting chapters in the history of Muslim 
thought The views of the school of Basra were first 
shaped by Abu Hashim (AD 933) and those of the 
school of Baghdad by that most exact and daring theo- 
logical thinker, Abu Bakar Bakilani (A D 1012) Later 
in the beginning of the thirteenth century we find a 
thoroughly systematic description in a book called the 
Guide of the Perplexed by Moses Mammonides—a Jew-

sities of Spain’ A French translation of this book was 
made by Munk in 1866, and recently Professor Mac-

contents in the Js1xs from which Dr Zwemer has re- 
printed it in the Mushm World of January 1928 Pro- 
fessor Macdonald, however, has made no attempt to

growth of atomistic ‘kalam’ in Islam He admits that 
there 1s nothing like the Atomism of Islam in Greek 
thought, but, unwilling as he 1s to give any credit for

Again we have seen that each atom occupies a posi-

The problem of time has always drawn the atten-

due partly to the fact that, according to the Quran, the

According to Muhyuddin Ibn-ul-Arabi, ‘Dahr’ 1s one of 
the beautiful names of God, and Razi tells us 1n his com-

‘Daithar The Ash‘arite theory of time 1s perhaps the 
first attempt 1n the history of Muslim thought to under- 
stand it philosophically Time, according to the 
'Ash‘arite 1s a succession of individual ‘nows’ From this 
view it obviously follows that between every two 
individual ‘nows’ or moments of time, there is an 
unoccupied moment of time, that is to say, a void 
of time The absurdity of this conclusion 1s due 
to the fact that they looked at the subject of their 
inquiry from a wholly objective point of view They 
took no lesson from the history of Greek thought, 
which had adopted the same point of view and had 
reached no results In our own time Newton describ- 
ed time as ‘something which in itself and from its own 
nature flows equally’ The metaphor of stream implied 
im this description suggests serious objections to 
Newton’s equally objective view of trme We cannot 
understand how a thing is affected on 1ts 1mmersion 1m 
this stream, and how it differs from things that do not 
participate in its flow Nor can we form any 1dea of 
the beginning, the end, and the boundaries of time 1f we

try to understand it on the analogy of a stream More-

The word knowledge, as applied to the finite ego, 
always means discursive knowledge—a temporal pro-

The universe, as we have seen before, 1s not an ‘other’

ego, and if there 1s nothing outside the Ultimate Ego, 
the Ultimate Ego cannot be conceived as an ego ‘The 
answer to this argument 1s that logical negations are of 
no use 1n forming a positive concept which must be 
based on the character of Reality as revealed in experi- 
ence Our criticism of experience reveals the Ultimate

our experience of life, cannot be conceived except as an

spontaneous and hence unforeseeable action 1s, in a 
sense, a limitation on the freedom of the all-inclusive 
Ego But this limitation 1s not externally 1mposed It 
1s born out of His own creative freedom whereby He 
has chosen finite egos to be participators of His Irfe, 
power, and freedom 

But how, 1t may be asked, 1s 1t possible to reconcile 
limitation with Omnipotence? The word limitation 

the Meaning of Prayer 8k 

need not frighten us The Quran has no liking for 
abstract universals It always fixes its gaze on the 
concrete which the theory of relativity has only recent- 
ly taught modern philosophy to see All activity, 
creational or otherwise, 1s a kind of limitation without 
which it 1s impossible to conceive God as a concrete 
operative Ego Omniupotence, abstractly conceived, 1s 
merely a blind, capricious power without limits The 
Quran has a clear and definite conception of Nature as 
a cosmos of mutually related forces It, therefore, 
views Divine omnipotence as intimately related to 
Divine wisdom, and finds the infinite power of God 
revealed not in the arbitrary and the capricious but 
in the recurrent, the regular, and the orderly At the 
same time, the Quran conceives God as ‘holding all 
goodness in His hands’ If, then the rationally directed 
Divine will 1s good, a very serious problem arises The 
course of evolution, as revealed by modern science, 
involves almost universal suffering and wrongdoing 
No doubt, wrongdoing 1s confined to man only But 
the fact of pain 1s almost universal, though 1t 1s equally 
true that men can suffer and have suffered the most 
excruciating pain for the sake of what they have believ- 
ed to be good Thus the two facts of moral and physi- 
cal evil stand out prominent 1n the life of Nature Nor 
can the relativity of evil and the presence of forces that 
tend to transmute 1t be a source of consolation to us, 
for 1n spite of all this relativity and transmutation there 
is something terribly positive about it How 18 it, then, 
possible to reconcile the goodness and omnipotence of 
God with the immense volume of evil in His creation? 
This painful problem is really the crux of Theism No 
modern writer has put it more accurately than 
Naumann in his Briefe uber Religion ‘We possess’ he 
says, ‘a knowledge of the world which teaches us a God 
of power and strength, who sends out life and death as 
simultaneously as shadow and light, and a revelation, 

$2 The Conception of God and 

a faith as to salvation which declares the same God to 
be father The following of the world-God produces 
the morality of the struggle for existence, and the 
service of the Father of Jesus Christ produces the 
morahty of compassion And yet they are not two 
gods, but one God Somehow or other, their arms 
intertwine Only no mortal can say where and how 
this occurs’ To the optimist Browning all 1s well with 
the world, to the pessimist Schopenhaure the world 1s 
one perpetual winter wherein a blind will expresses it- 
self in an infinite variety of living things which bemoan 
their emergence for a moment and then disappear for 
ever The issue thus raised between optimism and 
pessimism cannot be finally decided at the present stage 
of our knowledge of the universe Our intellectual 
constitution 1s such that we can take only a piecemeal 
view of things We cannot understand the full import 
of the great cosmic forces which work havoc, and at 
the same time sustain and amplify hfe The teaching 
of the Quran, which believes in the possibility of 
improvement in the behaviour of man and his control 
over natural forces, 1s neither optimism nor pessimism 
It 1s meliorism, which recognizes a growing universe 
and 1s animated by the hope of man’s eventual victory 
over evil 

But the clue to a better understanding of our 
difficulty 1s given in the legend relating to what 1s 
called the fall of man In this legend the Quran partly 
retains the ancient symbols, but the legend 1s materially 
transformed with a view to put an entirely fresh mean- 
ing intoit The Quranic method of complete or partial 
transformation of legends 1n order to besoul them with 
new ideas, and thus to adapt them to the advancing 
spirit of time, 1s an important point which has nearly 
always been overlooked both by Mushm and non- 
‘Muslim students of Islam The object of the Quran 

the Meaning of Prayer 83 

an dealing with these legends 1s seldom historical, 1t 
nearly always aims at giving them a universal moral or 
philosophical import And 1t achieves this object by 
omitting the names of persons and localities which tend 
to limit the meaning of a legend by giving it the colour 
of a specific historical event, and also by deleting details 
which appear to belong to a different order of feeling 
This 1s not an uncommon method of dealing with 
legends It 1s common 1n non-religious literature An 
instance 1n point 1s the legend of Faust, to which the 
touch of Goethe’s genius has given a wholly new 

meaning 

Turning to the legend of the Fall we find it in a 
variety of forms in the literatures of the ancient world 
It 1s, yndeed, impossible to demarcate the stages of its 
growth, and to set out clearly the various human 
motives which must have worked 1n its slow trans- 
formation But confining ourselves to the Semitic 
form of the myth, 1t 1s highly probable that it arose out 
of the primitive man’s desire to explain to himself the 
infinite misery of his plight in an uncongenial environ- 
ment, which abounded in disease and death and 
obstructed him on all sides in his endeavour to maintain 
himself Having no control over the forces of Nature, 
a pessimistic view of life was perfectly natural to 
him Thus, 1n an old Babylonian inscription, we find 
the serpent (phallic symbol), the tree, and the woman 
offering an apple (symbol of virginity) to the man 
The meaning of the myth is clear—the fall of man 
from a supposed state of bliss was due to the original 
sexual act of the human pair The way in which the 
Quran handles this legend becomes clear when we com- 
pare it with the narration of the Book of Genesis The 
remarkable points of difference between the Quranic 
and the Biblical narrations suggest unmistakably the 
purpose of the Quranic narration

Thus we see that the Quranic legend of the Fall

ity, 1t 1s man’s transition from simple consciousness

His Freedom and Immortality 99 

mental states Mental states do not exist in mutual

exist as phases of a complex whole, called mind The 
organic unity, however, of these inter-related states or, 
let us say, events 18 a special kind of unity It funda-

the parts of a material thing can exist in mutual 1sola-

For the body there can be but a single space The ego,

Another important characteristic of the unity of 
the ego 1s 1ts essential privacy which reveals the unique- 
ness of every ego In order to reach a certain conclu- 
sion all the premises of a syllogism must be believed

proposition ‘all men are mortal’, and another mind 

100 The Human Ego 

believes in the proposition ‘Socrates 1s a man’, no infer- 
ence 1s possible It 1s possible only 1f both the proposi- 
tions are believed in by me Again, my desire for a

not get the thing desired The dentist may sympathize

and resolutions, are exclusively mine God himself

another ego It 1s this unique inter-relation of our

William James conceives consciousness as ‘a stream of

something single, presupposed in all mental life, and not

element 1n experience There 1s no continuity of being

reason, an axiom of man’s moral consciousness Man 
demands and pursues the supreme good which com- 
prises both virtue and happiness But virtue and happ- 
ness, duty and inclination are, according to Kant, 
heterogeneous notions Their unity cannot be achiev- 
ed within the narrow span of the pursuer’s life in this 
sensible world We are therefore, driven to postulate 
immortal life for the person’s progressive completion of 
the unity of the mutually exclusive notions of virtue 
and happiness, and the existence of God eventually to 
effectuate this confluence It 1s not clear, however, why 
the consummation of virtue and happiness should take 
infinite time, and how God can effectuate the confluence 
between mutually exclusive notions This inconclusive- 

His Freedom and Immortality 113 

ness of metaphysical arguments had led many thinkers 
to confine themselves to meeting the objections of 
modern Materialism which rejects immortality, holding 
that consciousness 1s merely a function of the brain, and 
therefore ceases with the cessation of the brain-process 
William James thinks that this objection to immortality 
1s valid only 1f the function in question 1s taken to be 
productive The mere fact that certain mental changes 
vary concomitantly with certain bodily changes, does 
not warrant the inference that mental changes are pro- 
duced by bodily changes The function 1s not neces- 
sarily productive, 1t may be permissive or transmissive 
like the function of the trigger of a cross-bow or that of 
a reflecting lens This view which suggests that our 
inner life 1s due to the operation 1n us of a kind of tran- 
scendental mechanism of consciousness, somehow 
choosing a physical medium for a short period of sport, 
does not give us any assurance of the continuance of the 
content of our actual experience’ I have alreadv indi- 
cated 1n these lectures the proper way to meet Material- 
ism Science must necessarily select for study certain 
specihe aspects of Reality only and excludc others It 1s 
pure dogmatism on the part of science to claim that the 
aspects of Reality selected by it are the only aspects to 
be studied No doubt man hasa spatial aspect, but this 
is not the only aspect of man There are other aspects 
of man, such as evaluation, the unitary character of pur- 
posive experience, and the pursuit of truth which 
science must necessarily exclude from its study, and the 
understanding of which requires categories other than 
those employed by science 

There 1s, however, in the history of modern 
thought one positive view of immortality—I mean 
Nietzsche’s doctrine of Eternal Recurrence This 
view deserves some consideration, not only because 
Nietzsche has maintained it with a prophetical fer- 
vour, but also because it reveals a real tendency 

114 The Human Ego 

in the modern mind The idea occurred to several 
minds about the time when it came to Nietzsche ltkhe a 
poetic inspiration, and the germs of 1t are also found in 
Herbert Spencer It was really the power of the idea 
rather than its logical demonstration that appealed to 
this modern prophet This, 1n itself, is some evidence 
of the fact that positive views of ultimate things are the 
work rather of Inspiration than Metaphysics How- 
ever Nietzsche has given his doctrine the form of a 
reasoned out theory, and as such I think we are entitled 
to examine it The doctrine proceeds on the assumption 
that the quantity of energy 1n the universe 1s constant 
and consequently finite Space 1s only a subjective 
form, there is no meaning in saying that the world 1s 1n 
space mm the scnse that 1t 15 situated in an absolute empty 
void In his view of time, however, Nietzsche parts 
company with Kant and Schopenhauer Time 1s nota 
subjective form, it 1s a real and infinite process which 
can only be conceived as ‘periodic’ Thus itis clear that 
there cin be no dissipation of energy 1n an infinite empty 
space The centres of this energy are limited in number, 
and their combination perfectly calculable There 1s no 
beginning or end of this ever-active energy, no equili- 
brium, no first or last change Since time 1s infinite, 
therefore all possible combinations of energy-centres 
have already been exhausted There 1s no new hippen- 
ing in the universe, whatever happens now has happen- 
ed before an infinite number of times, and will continue 
to happen an infinite number of times in the future 
On Nietzsche s view the order of happenings in the 
universe must be fixed and unalterable, for since an in- 
finite time has passed, the energy-centres must have, 
by this time, formed certain definite modes of be- 
haviour The very word ‘Recurrence’ implies this fix- 
ity Further, we must conclude that a combination of 
energy-centres which has once taken place must always 
return, otherwise there would be no guarantee for the 

His Freedom and Immortality 115 

return even of the superman 
‘Everything has returned sirius and the spider, and thy

Such is Nietzsches Eternal Recurrence It is only

Nor does Nietzsche seriously grapple with the question

It 1s the expectation that a recurrence of the combina-

é UHAMMAD of Arabia ascended the highest

Man 1s primarily governed by passion and instinct = In-

Looking at the matter from this point of view,

and experience in the Quran, and the emphasis that it

The idea, however, does not mean that mystic experi-

The Spirit of Muslim Culture 127 

and ‘Afaq’ (world) as sources of knowledge God re-

. But inner experience 1s only one source of human

136 The Sperit of Muslim Culture 

immaterial beings, and the space of God The space of 
material bodies 1s further divided into three kinds First, 
the space of gross bodies of which we predicate room1- 
ness In this space movement takes time, bodies occupy 
their respective places and resist displacement Second- 
ly, the space of subtle bodies, e g , air and sound In this 
space too bodies resist each other, and their movement

different to the space of air and sound There 1s, how-

displacing the air in the room, and this shows that the

has no entry into the space of hght In view of the 
close proximity of these spaces however 1t 1s not pos-

purely intellectual analysis and spiritual experience 

Again in the hot water the two opposites—fire and 
water—which appear to interpenetrate each other can-

space The fact cannot be explained except on the sup- 
position that the spaces of the two substances, though 
closely proximate to each other are nevertheless distinct 

But while the element of distance 1s not entirely absent, 
there is no possibility of mutual resistance 1n the space 
of light The light of a candle reaches up to a certain 
point only, and the lights of a hundred candles inter- 
mingle in the same room without displacing one 
another

THE PRINCIPLE OF MOVEMENT IN THE 
STRUCTURE OF ISLAM 

S a cultural movement Islam rejects the old static 
view of the universe, and reaches a dynamic view 

As an emotional system of unification it recognizes the 
worth of the individual as such, and rejects blood-rela- 
tionship as a basis of human unity Blood-relation- 
ship 1s earth-rootedness The search for a purely psy- 
chological foundation of human unity becomes possible 
only with the perception that all human life 1s spiritual 
in its origin Such a perception 1s creative of fresh 
loyalties without any ceremonial to keep them alive, 
and makes it possible for man to emancipate himself 
from the earth Christianity which had originally 
appeared as a monastic order was tried by Constantine 
as a system of unification Its failure to work as such 
a system arove the Emperor Julian to return to the old 
gods of Rome on which he attempted to put philosophi- 
cal interpretations A modern historian of civilization 
has thus depicted the state of the civilized world about 
the time when Islam appeared on the stage of History 

‘It seemed then that the great civilization that it had 
taken four thousand years to construct was on the verge of 
disintegration and that mankind was likely to return to that 
condition of barbarism where every tribe and sect was against 
the next, and law and order were unknown The old tribal 
sanctions had lost their power Hence the old imperial 
methods would no longer operate The new sanctions created 
‘by Christianity were working division and destruction instead 
of unity and order It was a time fraught with tragedy Civil- 
ization, like a gigantic tree whose foliage had overarched the 
world and whose branches had borne the golden fruits of art 
and science and literature, stood tottering, its trunk no longer 

the Structure of Islam 147 

alive with the flowing sap of devotion and reverence, but rot- 
ted to the core, driven by the storms of war, and held together 
only by the cords of ancient customs and laws, that might snap 
at any moment Was there any emotional culture that could 
be brought 1n to gather mankind once more into unity and to 
save civilization® This culture must be something of a new 
type, for the old sanctions and ceremonials were dead, and to 
build up others of the same kind would be the work of 
centuries ’ 

The writer then proceeds to tell us that the world 
stood in need of a new culture to take the place of the 
culture of the throne, and the systems of unification 
which were based on blood-relationship It 1s amazing, 
he adds, that such culture should have arisen from 
Arabia just at the time when 1t was most needed There 
is, however, nothing amazing in the phenomenon The 
world-life intuitively sees 1ts own needs, and at critical 
moments defines its own direction This 1s what, 
in the language of religion, we call prophetic 
revelation It 1s only natural that Islam _ should 
have flashed across the consciousness of a simple 
people untouched by any of the ancient cultures, and 
occupying a geographical position where three conti- 
nents meet together The new culture finds the founda- 
tion of world-unity mm the principle of “Tauhid’ Islam, 
asa polity, 1s only a practical means of making this 
principle a living factor 1n the intellectual and emotional 
life of mankind It demands loyalty to God, not to 
thrones And since God 1s the ultimate spiritual basis 
of all life, loyalty to God virtually amounts to man’s 
loyalty to his own 1deal nature The ultimate spiritual 
basis of all life, as conceived by Islam, 1s eternal and re- 
veals itself in variety and change A society based on 
such a conception of Reality must reconcile, in its Infe, 
the categories of permanence and change It must 
possess eternal principles to regulate its collective life, 

148 The Principle of Movement tn 

for the eternal gives us a foothold in the world of per- 
petual change But eternal principles when they are 
understood to exclude all possibilities of change which, 
according to the Quran, 1s one of the greatest ‘signs’ of 
God, tend to immobilize what 1s essentially mobile 1n 1ts 
nature The failure of Europe in political and social 
science illustrates the former principle, the immobility 
of Islam during the last 500 years illustrates the latter 
What then 1s the principle of movement 1n the structure 
of Islam? This 1s known as ‘Tjtthad’ 

The word literally means to exert In the termino- 
logy of Islamic law it means to exert with a view to form 
an independent judgement on a legal question The idea, 
I believe, has its origin in a well-known verse of the 
Quian—‘And to those who exert We show Our path’ 
We hind it more definitely adumbrated 1n a tradition of 
the Holy Prophet When Ma‘ad was appointed ruler 
of Yemen, the Piophet 1s reported to have asked him 
as to how he would decide matters coming up before 
him ‘TI will judge matters according to the Book of 
God,’ said Ma‘ad ‘But if the Book of God contains 
nothing to guide you?’ “Then I will act on the prece- 
dents of the Prophet of God’ ‘But if the precedents 
fail?’ ‘Then I will exert to form my own judgement’ 
The student of the history of Islam, however, 1s well 
aware that with the political expansion of Islam system- 
atic legal thought became an absolute necessity, and our 
early doctors of law, both of Arabian and non-Arabian 
descent, worked ceaselessly until all the accumulated 
wealth of legal thought found a final expression 1n our 
recognized schools of Law These schools of law recog- 
nize three degrees of Ijtihad (1) complete authority in 
legislation which 1s practically confined to the founders 
of the schools, (2) relative authority which is to be 
exercised within the limits of a particular school, and 
(3) special authority which relates to the determining 

the Structure of Islam 149 

of the law applicable to a particular case left undeter- 
mined by the founders In this paper 1 am concerned 
with the first degree of Ijtihad only, 1e, complete 
authority 1n legislation The theoretical possibility of 
this degree of Ijtihad 1s admitted by the Sunnis, but in 
practice it has always been denied ever since the estab- 
lishment of the schools, inasmuch as the idea of com- 
plete Ijtithad 1s hedged round by conditions which are 
wellnigh impossible of realization 1n a single individual 
Such an attitude seems exceedingly strange in a system 
of law based mainly on the groundwork provided by the 
Quran which embodies an essentially dynamic outlook 
on life It 1s, therefore, necessary, beforc we proceed 
farther, to discover the causes of this intellectual atti- 
tude which has reduced the Law of Islam practically to 
a state of uzmmobility Some European writers think 
that the stationary character of the Law of Islam 1s due 
to the influence of the Turks This 1s an entirely super- 
ficial view, for the legal schools of Islam had been final- 
ly established long before the Turkish influence began 
to work 1n the history of Islam The real causes are, in 
my opinion, as follows 

1 Weare all familiar with the Rationalist move- 
ment which appeared 1n the church of Islam during the 
early days of the Abbasides, and the bitter controversies 
which it raised Take for instance the one important 
point of controversy between the two camps—the con- 
servative dogma of the eternity of the Quran The Ra- 
tionafists denied it because they thought that this was 
only another form of the Christian dogma of the etern- 
ity of the word, on the other hand, the conservative 
thinkers whom the later Abbasides, fearing the political 
implications of Rationalism, gave their support, thought 
that by denying the eternity of the Quran the Rational- 
ists were undermining the very foundations of Muslin 
society Nazzam, for instance, practically rejected the 

150 The Principle of Movement in 

traditions, and openly declared Abu Huraira to be an 
untrustworthy reporter Thus, partly owing to a mis- 
understanding of the ultimate motives of Rationalism, 
and partly owing to the unrestrained thought of parti- 
cular Rationalists, conservative thinkers regarded this 
movement as a force of disintegration, and considered 1t 
a danger to the stability of Islam as a social polity Their 
main purpose, therefore, was to preserve the social 1n- 
tegrity of Islam, and to realize this the only course open 
to them was to utilize the binding force of Shari‘at, and 
to make the structure of their legal system as rigorous 
as possible 

2 The rise and growth of ascetic Sufiism, which 
gradually developed under influences of a non-Islamic 
character, a purely speculative side, 1s to a large extent 
responsible for this attitude On its purely religious 
side Sufiism fostered a hind of revolt against the verbal 
quibbles of our early doctors The case of Sufyan Saur1 
is an instance in point He was one of the acutest legal 
minds of his time, and was nearly the founder of a 
school of law, but being also intensely spiritual, the dry- 
as-dust subtleties of contemporary legists drove him to 
ascetic Sufiism On 1ts speculative side which develop- 
ed later, Sufiism 1s a form of freethought and 1n alliance 
with Rationalism The emphasis that it laid on the 
distinction of zalur and batin (Appearance and Reality} 
created an attitude of indifference to all that applies to 
Appearance and not to Reality 

This spirit of total other-worldliness 1n later Sufism 
obscured men’s vision of a very important aspect of 
Islam as a social polity, and offering the prospect of un- 
restrained thought on its speculative side 1t attracted 
and finally absorbed the best minds in Islam The 
Muslim state was thus left generally in the hands of 
wntellectual mediocrities, and the unthinking masses of 
Islam, having no personalities of a higher calibre to 

the Structure of Islam 151 

guide them, found their security only 1n blindly follow- 
ing the schools 

3 On the top of all this came the destruction of 
Baghdad—the centre of Muslim intellectual life—in 
the middle of the thirteenth century This was indeed 
a great blow, and all the contemporary historians of the 
invasion of Tartars describe the havoc of Baghdad with 
a half-suppressed pessimism about the future of Islam 
For fear of further disintegration, which 1s only natural 
in such a period of political decay, the conservative 
thinkers of Islam focussed all their efforts on the one 
point of preserving a uniform social life for the people 
by a jealous exclusion of all innovations 1n the law of 
Shari‘at as expounded by the early doctors of Islam 
Their leading idea was social order, and there 1s no doubt 
that they were partly right, because organization does. 
to a certain extent counteract the forces of decay But 
they did not see, and our modern Ulema do not see, that 
the ultimate fate of a people does not depend so much 
on organization as on the worth and power of individual 
men In an over-organized society the individual 1s 
altogether crushed out of existence He gains the 
whole wealth of social thought around him and loses his 
own soul Thus a false reveience for past history and 
its artificial resurrection constitute no remedy for a 
people’s decay “The verdict of history’, as a modern 
writer has happily put it, ‘is that worn out ideas have 
never risen to power among a people who have worn 
them out’ The only effective power, therefore, that 
counteracts the forces of decay 1n a people 1s the rearing 
of self-concentrated individuals Such individuals 
alone reveal the depth of life They disclose new stand- 
ards in the light of which we begin to see that our en- 
vironment 1s not wholly inviolable and requires revision 
The tendency to over-organization by a false reverence 
of the past as manifested 1n the legists of Islam 1n the 
thirteenth century and later, was contrary to the inner

The truth 1s that among the Mushm nations of 
to-day, Turkey alone has shaken off 1ts dogmatic slum- 
ber, and attained to self-consciousness She alone has 
claimed her right of intellectual freedom, she alone has 
passed from the ideal to the real—a transition which

growing complexities of a mobile and broadening life 
are sure to bring new situations suggesting new pots 
of view, and necessitating fresh interpretations of prin- 
ciples which are only of an academic interest to a people 
who have never experienced the joy of spiritual expan- 
sion Its, I think, the English thinker Hobbes who 
makes this acute observation that to have a succession 
of identical thoughts and feelings 1s to have no thoughts 
and feelings at all Such 1s the lot of most Muslim 
countries to-day They are mechanically repeating old 
values, whereas the Turk is on the way to creating new 
values He has passed through great experiences which 
have revealed his deeper self to him In him life has 
begun to move, change, 1nd amplify, giving birth to 
new desires, bringing new difficulties and suggesting, 
new interpretations The question which confronts 
him to-day, and which is likely to confront other 
Muslim countries in the near future 1s whether the Law

We heartily welcome the liberal movement in 
modern Islam, but 1t must also be admitted that the

modern Islam with greater force than ever may ulti-

Muslim people have imbibed from their religion 
Further, our religious and political reformers in their

reform in the absence of a check on their youthful 
fervour We are to-day passing through a period 
similar to that of the Protestant revolution in Europe, 
and the lesson which the rise and outcome of Luther’s 
movement teaches should not be lost onus’ A careful 
reading of history shows that the Reformation was 
essentially a political movement, and the net result of it 
in Europe was a gradual displacement of the universal 
ethics of Christianity by systems of national ethics The 
result of this tendency we have seen with our own eyes

ethics, has made the European situation still more 1ntol- 
erable It is the duty of the leaders of the world of 
Islam to-day to understand the real meaning of what 
has happened 1n Europe, and then to move forward with 
self-control and a clear insight into the ultimate aims of 
Islam as a social polity

entirely, for it 1s their past that has made their personal

With regard to the Turkish poet’s demand, I am 
afraid he does not seem to know much about the family

Marriage, according to Mohammedan Law, 1s a civil 
contract The wife at the time of marriage 1s at liberty 
to get the husband’s power of divorce delegated to her

The share of the daughter 1s determined not by any 
inferiority inherent in her, but in view of her economic

170 The Principle of Movement in 

in the distribution of wealth, but as one factor among

(d) The Qtyas The fourth basis of Fiqh 1s Qryas, 
1e, the use of analogical reasoning in legislation In 
view of different social and agricultural conditions pre- 
vailing in the countries conquered by Islam, the school 
of Abu Hanifa seem to have found, on the whole, little 
or no guidance from the precedents recorded 1n the 
literature of traditions The only alternative open to 
them was to resort to speculative reason 1n their inter- 
pretations The application of Aristotelian logic, how- 
ever, though suggested by the discovery of new condi- 
tions 1n Iraq, was likely to prove exceedingly harmful 1n 
the preliminary stages of legal development The intri- 
cate behaviour of life cannot be subjected to hard and 
fast rules logically deducible from certain general 
notions Yet looked at through the spectacles of 
Aristotle’s logic 1t appears to be a mechanism pure and 
simple with no internal principle of movement Thus 
the school of Abu Hanifa tended to ignore the creative 
freedom and arbitrariness of life, and hoped to build a 
logically perfect legal system on the lines of pure reason 
The legists of Hedjaz, however, true to the practical 
genius of their race, raised strong protests against the 
scholastic subtleties of the legists of Iraq, and their 
tendency to imagine unreal cases which they 11ghtly 
thought would turn the Law of Islam into a kind of life- 
less mechanism These bitter controversies among the 
early doctors of Islam led to a critical definition of the 
limitations, conditions, and correctives of Q1yas which, 
though originally appeared as a mere disguise for the 
Muyjtahid’s personal opinion, eventually became a source 

the Structure of Islam 1%7 

of life and movement 1n the law of Islam The spirit 
of the acute criticism of Malik and Shafi‘: on Abu 
Hanifa’s principle of Qiyas, as a source of law, consti- 
tutes really an effective Semitic restraint on the Aryan 
tendency to seize the abstract in preference to the 
concrete, to enjoy the idea rather than the event This 
was really a controversy between the advocates of 
deductive and inductive methods 1n legal research The 
legists of Iraq originally emphasized the eternal aspect 
of the ‘notion’, while those of Hedyjaz laid stress on 1ts 
temporal aspect The latter, however, did not see the 
full significance of their own position, and their instinc- 
tive partiality to the legal tradition of Hedjaz narrowed 
their vision to the ‘precedents’ that had actually happen- 
edin the days of the Prophet and his companions 
No doubt they recognized the value of the concrete, but 
at the same time they eternalized it, rarely resorting to

IS RELIGION POSSIBLE? 

Breeeet speaking religious life may be divided 
into three periods ‘These may be described as 
the periods of ‘Faith’, ‘Thought’, and ‘Discovery’ In 
the first period religious life appears as a form of disc1- 
pline which the individual or a whole people must 
accept as an unconditional command without any 
rational understanding of the ultimate meaning and 
purpose of that command This attitude may be of great 
consequence in the social and political history of a 
people, but 1s not of much consequence 1n so far as the 
individual’s inner growth and expansion are concerned 
Perfect submission to discipline 1s followed by a 
rational understanding of the discipline and the ultimate 
source of 1ts authority In this period religious life 
seeks 1ts foundation 1n a kind of metaphysics—a logical- 
ly consistent view of the world with God as a part of 
that view In the third period metaphysics 1s displaced 
by psychology, and religious life develops the ambition 
to come into direct contact with the ultimate Reality 
It is here that religion becomes a matter of personal 
assimilation of life and power, and the individual 
achieves a free personality, not by releasing himself 
from the fetters of the law, but by discovering the ult1- 
mate source of the law within the depths of his own con- 
sciousness As 1n the words of a Muslim Sufi—‘no 
understanding of the Holy Book 1s possible until 1t 1s 
actually revealed to the believer just as 1t was revealed 
to the Prophet’ Its, then, in the sense of this last 
phase 1n the development of religious life that I use the 
word religion in the question that I now propose to

As we all know, 1t was Kant who first raised the 
question ‘Is metaphysics possible?’ He answered this 
question in the negative, and his argument applies with 
equal force to the realities in which religion 1s especially 
interested The manifold of sense, according to him,

idea Its function 1s merely regulative If there 1s 
some actuality corresponding to the idea 1t falls outside 
the boundaries of experience, and consequently its 
existence cannot be rationally demonstrated This 
verdict of Kant cannot be easily accepted It may fair-

him to the real sigmificance of his vision Instead of 
looking for a spiritual rule which would develop the 
Divine even 1n a plebeian and thus open up before him 
an infinite future, Nietzsche was driven to seek the real- 
ization of his vision in such schemes as aristocratic 
radicalism As I have said of him elsewhere 
The ‘I am’ which he seeketh, 
Lieth beyond philosophy, beyond knowledge 
The plant that groweth only from the invisible soil of the heart 
of man, 
Groweth not from a mere heap of clay! 

Thus failed a genius whose vision was solely deter- 
mined by his internal forces, and remained unproduc- 
tive for want of expert external guidance 1n his spiritual 
life And the irony of fate 1s that this man, who 
appeared to his friends ‘as 1f he had come from a coun- 
try where no man lived,’ was fully conscious of his great 
spiritual need ‘I confront alone,’ he says, ‘an immense 
problem 1t 1s as 1f I am lost 1n a forest, a primeval one 
I need help Ineed disciples I need a master It would 
be so sweet to obey’ Andagain ‘Why do I not find 

Is Religion Posstble? 195 

among the living men who see higher than I do and 
have to look down on me? Is it only that I have made 
a poor search? And I have so great a longing for such’ 

The truth 1s that the religious and the scientific 
processes, though involving different methods, are 
identical in their final aim Both aim at reaching the 
most real In fact, religion, for reasons which I have 
mentioned before, 1s far more anxious to reach the ulti- 
mately real than science And to both the way to pure 
objectivity lies through what may be called the purifi- 
cation of experience In order to understand this we 
must make a distinction between experience as a natural 
fact, significant of the normally observable behaviour 
of reality, and experience as significant of the inner 
nature of reality Asa natural fact 1t 1s explained in the 
light of its antecedents, psychological and physiological, 
as significant of the inner nature of reality we shall have 
to apply criteria of a different kind to clarify its mean- 
ing In the domain of science we try to understand 1ts 
meaning in reference to the external behaviour of real- 
ity, 1n the domain of religion we take 1t as representa- 
tive of some kind of reality and try to discover 1ts mean- 
ings in reference mainly to the inner nature of that real- 
ity The scientific and the religious processes are in a 
sense parallel to each other Both are really descrip- 
tions of the same world with this difference only that 1n 
the scientific process the ego’s standpoint 1s necessarily 
exclusive whereas in the religious process the ego 
integrates 1ts competing tendencies and develops a 
single inclusive attitude resulting 1n a kind of synthetic 
transfiguration of his experiences A careful study of 
the nature and purpose of these really complementary 
processes shows that both of them are directed to the 
purification of experience in their respective spheres 
An illustration will make my meaning clear Hume’s 
criticism of our notion of cause must be considered as a 

196 Is Religion Possible? 

chapter in the history of science rather than that of 
philosophy ‘True to the spirit of scientific empiricism 
we are not entitled to work with any concepts of a sub- 
jective nature The point of Hume’s criticism 1s to 
emancipate empirical science from the concept of force 
which, as he urges, has no foundation 1n sense-exper1- 
ence This was the first attempt of the modern mind 
to purify the scientific process 

E:nstein’s mathematical view of the universe com- 
pletes the process of purification started by Hume, and, 
true to the spirit of Hume’s criticism, dispenses with the 
concept of force altogether The passage I have quoted 
from the great Indian saint shows that the practical 
student of religious psychology has a similar purifica- 
tion in view His sense of objectivity 1s as keen as that 
of the scientist in his own sphere of objectivity He 
passes from experience to experience, not as a mere 
spectator, but as a critical sifter of experience who by 
the rules of a peculiar technique, suited to his sphere of 
inquiry, endeavours to eliminate all subjective elements, 
psychological or physiological, in the content of his 
experience with a view finally to reach what 1s absolute- 
ly objective This final experience 1s the revelation of 
a new life-process—original, essential, spontaneous 
The eternal secret of the ego is that the moment he 
reaches this final revelation he recognizes i:t as the ulti- 
mate root of his being without the slightest hesitation 
Yet in the experience itself there is no mystery Nor 1s 
there anything emotional in it Indeed with a view to 
secure a wholly non-emotional experience the technique 
of Islamic Sufiism at least takes good care to forbid the 
use of music 1n worship, and to emphasize the necessity 
of daily congregational prayers 1n order to counteract 
the possible anti-social effects of solitary contemplation 
Thus the experience reached is a perfectly natural 
experience and possesses a biological significance of 

Is Religion Possible? 197 

the highest 1mportance to the ego It 1s the human 
ego rising higher than mere reflection, and mend- 
ine its transiency by appropriating the eternal 

The only danger to which the ego 1s exposed in 
this Divine quest is the possible relaxation of his 
activity caused by his enjoyment of and absorption in 
the experiences that precede the final experience The 
history of Eastern Sufism shows that this 1s a real 
danger This was the whole point of the reform move- 
ment initiated by the great Indian saint from whose 
writings I have already quoted a passage And the 
reason 1s obvious The ultimate aim of the ego 1s not to 
see something, but to be something It 1s 1n the ego’s 
effort to be something that he discovers his final oppor- 
tunity to sharpen his objectivity and acquire a more 
fundamental ‘I am’ which finds evidence of :ts reality 
not 1n the Cartesian ‘TI think’ but in the Kantian ‘T can’ 

The end of the ego’s quest is not emancipation from the 
limitations of individuality, it 1s, on the other hand, a 
more precise definition of 1t The final act 1s not an 
intellectual act, but a vital act which deepens the whole 
being of the ego, and sharpens his wii' with the creative 
assurance that the world 1s not something to be merely 
seen or known through concepts, but something to be 
made and re-made by continuous action It 1s a moment 

of supreme bliss and also a moment of the greatest trial 
for the ego 

Art thou 1n the stage of ‘life’, ‘death’, or ‘death-in life’? 
Invoke the aid of three witnesses to verify thy ‘Station’ 
‘The first witness 1s thine own consciousness— 

See thyself, then with thine own light 

The second witness 1s the consciousness of another ego 
See thyself, then, with the light of an ego other than thee 
The third witness is God’s consciousness— 

See thyself, then, with God’s light 

If thou standest unshaken in front of this light, 

198 Is Religion Posstble? 

Consider thyself as living and eternal as He! 
That man alone 1s real who dares— 

Dares to see God face to face! 

What 1s ‘Ascension’? Only a search for a witness 
Who may finally confirm thy reality— 

A witness whose confirmation alone makes thee eterna). 
No one can stand unshaken in His Presence, 
And he who can, verily, he 1s pure gold 

Art thou a mere particle of dust? 

Tighten the knot of thy ego, 

And hold fast to thy tiny being! 

How glorious to burnish one’s ego 

And to test its lustre in the presence of the Sun! 
Re-chisel, then, thine ancient frame, 

And build up a new being 

Such being 1s real being, 

Or else thy ego 1s a mere ring of smoke! 
Jawid Nama
